	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Royal Society</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">National academy of science in the United Kingdom</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the national academy of science in the United Kingdom. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Royal Society (disambiguation)">Royal Society (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">


</p>
<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn org">The Royal Society of London for Improving Natural Knowledge</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Royal_Society_Coat_of_Arms.svg" class="image"><img alt="The Royal Society Coat of Arms.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/The_Royal_Society_Coat_of_Arms.svg/200px-The_Royal_Society_Coat_of_Arms.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="207" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/The_Royal_Society_Coat_of_Arms.svg/300px-The_Royal_Society_Coat_of_Arms.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/The_Royal_Society_Coat_of_Arms.svg/400px-The_Royal_Society_Coat_of_Arms.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="302" data-file-height="313" /></a><div><a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">Coat of arms</a> of the Royal Society. <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Society_entrance.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Royal_Society_entrance.jpg/220px-Royal_Society_entrance.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Royal_Society_entrance.jpg/330px-Royal_Society_entrance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Royal_Society_entrance.jpg/440px-Royal_Society_entrance.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3596" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Society_entrance.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Entrance to the Royal Society at 6–9 <a href="/wiki/Carlton_House_Terrace" title="Carlton House Terrace">Carlton House Terrace</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p><b>The Royal Society</b>, formally <b>The Royal Society of London for Improving Natural Knowledge</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-royalsociety.org_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royalsociety.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Learned_society" title="Learned society">learned society</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>'s national <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy of Sciences">Academy of Sciences</a>. Founded on 28 November 1660, it was granted a <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">royal charter</a> by <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King Charles II</a> as "The Royal Society".<sup id="cite_ref-royalsociety.org_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royalsociety.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It is the oldest national scientific institution in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The society fulfils a number of roles: promoting science and its benefits, recognising excellence in science, supporting outstanding science, providing scientific advice for policy, fostering international and global co-operation, education and public engagement.  It also performs these roles for the smaller countries of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>.
</p><p>The society is governed by its Council, which is chaired by the Society's President, according to a set of statutes and standing orders. The members of Council and the President are elected from and by its Fellows, the basic members of the society, who are themselves elected by existing Fellows. As of 2016, there are about 1,600 fellows, allowed to use the postnominal title FRS (<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">Fellow of the Royal Society</a>), with up to 52 new fellows appointed each year. There are also royal fellows, honorary fellows and foreign members, the last of which are allowed to use the postnominal title ForMemRS (Foreign Member of the Royal Society). The Royal Society President is <a href="/wiki/Venkatraman_Ramakrishnan" title="Venkatraman Ramakrishnan">Venkatraman Ramakrishnan</a>, who took up the post on 30 November 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 1967, the society has been based at 6–9 <a href="/wiki/Carlton_House_Terrace" title="Carlton House Terrace">Carlton House Terrace</a>, a Grade I <a href="/wiki/Listed_building" title="Listed building">listed building</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">central London</a> which was previously used by the <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_Germany,_London" title="Embassy of Germany, London">Embassy of Germany</a>, London.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_and_early_years">Founding and early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Founding and early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gresham_College_and_the_formation_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Gresham College and the formation of the Royal Society">Gresham College and the formation of the Royal Society</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Invisible_College" title="Invisible College">Invisible College</a> has been described as a precursor group to the Royal Society of London, consisting of a number of <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosopher" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural philosopher">natural philosophers</a> around <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>. The concept of "invisible college" is mentioned in German <a href="/wiki/Rosicrucian" class="mw-redirect" title="Rosicrucian">Rosicrucian</a> pamphlets in the early 17th century. <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a> in England referenced the idea, related in meaning to <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Salomon%27s_House" title="Salomon&#39;s House">House of Solomon</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/Masque" title="Masque">masque</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fortunate_Isles_and_Their_Union" title="The Fortunate Isles and Their Union">The Fortunate Isles and Their Union</a></i> from 1624/5.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The term accrued currency for the exchanges of correspondence within the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Letters" title="Republic of Letters">Republic of Letters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
In letters in 1646 and 1647, Boyle refers to "our invisible college" or "our philosophical college". The society's common theme was to acquire knowledge through experimental investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Three dated letters are the basic documentary evidence: Boyle sent them to Isaac Marcombes (Boyle's former tutor and a <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a>, who was then in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>), <a href="/wiki/Francis_Tallents" title="Francis Tallents">Francis Tallents</a> who at that point was a fellow of <a href="/wiki/Magdalene_College,_Cambridge" title="Magdalene College, Cambridge">Magdalene College, Cambridge</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and London-based <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hartlib" title="Samuel Hartlib">Samuel Hartlib</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Purver_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purver-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JohnEvelyn1687.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/JohnEvelyn1687.jpg/220px-JohnEvelyn1687.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/JohnEvelyn1687.jpg/330px-JohnEvelyn1687.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/JohnEvelyn1687.jpg/440px-JohnEvelyn1687.jpg 2x" data-file-width="868" data-file-height="944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JohnEvelyn1687.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Evelyn" title="John Evelyn">John Evelyn</a>, who helped to found the Royal Society.</div></div></div>
<p>The Royal Society started from groups of physicians and <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosopher" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural philosopher">natural philosophers</a>, meeting at a variety of locations, including <a href="/wiki/Gresham_College" title="Gresham College">Gresham College</a> in London. They were influenced by the "<a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">new science</a>", as promoted by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/New_Atlantis" title="New Atlantis">New Atlantis</a></i>, from approximately 1645 onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> A group known as "The Philosophical Society of Oxford" was run under a set of rules still retained by the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sy1s_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sy1s-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">English Restoration</a>, there were regular meetings at Gresham College.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It is widely held that these groups were the inspiration for the foundation of the Royal Society.<sup id="cite_ref-sy1s_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sy1s-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another view of the founding, held at the time, was that it was due to the influence of French scientists and the <a href="/wiki/Montmor_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Montmor Academy">Montmor Academy</a> in 1657, reports of which were sent back to England by English scientists attending. This view was held by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_du_Hamel" title="Jean-Baptiste du Hamel">Jean-Baptiste du Hamel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Domenico Cassini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_le_Bovier_de_Fontenelle" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernard le Bovier de Fontenelle">Bernard le Bovier de Fontenelle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melchis%C3%A9dech_Th%C3%A9venot" title="Melchisédech Thévenot">Melchisédech Thévenot</a> at the time and has some grounding in that <a href="/wiki/Henry_Oldenburg" title="Henry Oldenburg">Henry Oldenburg</a>, the society's first secretary, had attended the Montmor Academy meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a>, however, disputed this, writing that:
</p>
<blockquote><p>[Cassini] makes, then, Mr Oldenburg to have been the instrument, who inspired the English with a desire to imitate the French, in having Philosophical Clubs, or Meetings; and that this was the occasion of founding the Royal Society, and making the French the first. I will not say, that Mr Oldenburg did rather inspire the French to follow the English, or, at least, did help them, and hinder us. But 'tis well known who were the principal men that began and promoted that design, both in this city and in Oxford; and that a long while before Mr Oldenburg came into England. And not only these Philosophic Meetings were before Mr Oldenburg came from Paris; but the Society itself was begun before he came hither; and those who then knew Mr Oldenburg, understood well enough how little he himself knew of philosophic matter.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RoyalSocMace20040420.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/RoyalSocMace20040420.jpg/220px-RoyalSocMace20040420.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="71" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/RoyalSocMace20040420.jpg/330px-RoyalSocMace20040420.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/RoyalSocMace20040420.jpg/440px-RoyalSocMace20040420.jpg 2x" data-file-width="616" data-file-height="199" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RoyalSocMace20040420.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mace granted by Charles II.</div></div></div>
<p>On 28 November 1660, the <a href="/wiki/Gresham_College_and_the_formation_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Gresham College and the formation of the Royal Society">1660 committee of 12</a> announced the formation of a "College for the Promoting of Physico-Mathematical Experimental Learning", which would meet weekly to discuss science and run experiments. At the second meeting, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Moray" title="Robert Moray">Sir Robert Moray</a> announced that the <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King</a> approved of the gatherings, and a <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">royal charter</a> was signed on 15 July 1662 which created the "Royal Society of London", with <a href="/wiki/William_Brouncker,_2nd_Viscount_Brouncker" title="William Brouncker, 2nd Viscount Brouncker">Lord Brouncker</a> serving as the first president. A second royal charter was signed on 23 April 1663, with the king noted as the founder and with the name of "the Royal Society of London for the Improvement of Natural Knowledge"; Robert Hooke was appointed as Curator of Experiments in November. This initial royal favour has continued and, since then, every monarch has been the patron of the society.<sup id="cite_ref-pw_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pw-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The society's early meetings included experiments performed first by Hooke and then by <a href="/wiki/Denis_Papin" title="Denis Papin">Denis Papin</a>, who was appointed in 1684. These experiments varied in their subject area, and were both important in some cases and trivial in others.<sup id="cite_ref-hen1_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hen1-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The society also published an English translation of <i>Essays of Natural Experiments Made in the Accademia del Cimento, under the Protection of the Most Serene Prince Leopold of Tuscany</i> in 1684, an Italian book documenting experiments at the <a href="/wiki/Accademia_del_Cimento" title="Accademia del Cimento">Accademia del Cimento</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Although meeting at Gresham College, the Society temporarily moved to <a href="/wiki/Arundel_House" title="Arundel House">Arundel House</a> in 1666 after the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_London" title="Great Fire of London">Great Fire of London</a>, which did not harm Gresham but did lead to its appropriation by the Lord Mayor. The Society returned to Gresham in 1673.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There had been an attempt in 1667 to establish a permanent "college" for the society. Michael Hunter argues that this was influenced by "<a href="/wiki/Solomon%27s_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Solomon&#39;s House">Solomon's House</a>" in Bacon's <i>New Atlantis</i> and, to a lesser extent, by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Valentinus_Andreae" title="Johannes Valentinus Andreae">J. V. Andreae</a>'s <i>Christianopolis</i>, dedicated research institutes, rather than the colleges at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge</a>, since the founders only intended for the society to act as a location for research and discussion. The first proposal was given by <a href="/wiki/John_Evelyn" title="John Evelyn">John Evelyn</a> to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> in a letter dated 3 September 1659; he suggested a grander scheme, with apartments for members and a central research institute. Similar schemes were expounded by <a href="/wiki/Bengt_Skytte" title="Bengt Skytte">Bengt Skytte</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Cowley" title="Abraham Cowley">Abraham Cowley</a>, who wrote in his <i>Proposition for the Advancement of Experimental Philosophy</i> in 1661 of a "'Philosophical College", with houses, a library and a chapel. The society's ideas were simpler and only included residences for a handful of staff, but Hunter maintains an influence from Cowley and Skytte's ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Henry Oldenburg and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sprat" title="Thomas Sprat">Thomas Sprat</a> put forward plans in 1667 and Oldenburg's co-secretary, <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkins" title="John Wilkins">John Wilkins</a>, moved in a council meeting on 30 September 1667 to appoint a committee "for raising contributions among the members of the society, in order to build a college".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> These plans were progressing by November 1667, but never came to anything, given the lack of contributions from members and the "unrealised—perhaps unrealistic"—aspirations of the society.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_century">18th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 18th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/170px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/255px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/340px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 2x" data-file-width="407" data-file-height="559" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sir_Isaac_Newton" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a> FRS, President of Royal Society, 1703–1727. Newton was one of the earliest Fellows of the Royal Society, elected in 1672.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2ndEarlOfHardwicke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/2ndEarlOfHardwicke.jpg/170px-2ndEarlOfHardwicke.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/2ndEarlOfHardwicke.jpg/255px-2ndEarlOfHardwicke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/2ndEarlOfHardwicke.jpg/340px-2ndEarlOfHardwicke.jpg 2x" data-file-width="369" data-file-height="458" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2ndEarlOfHardwicke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Philip_Yorke,_2nd_Earl_of_Hardwicke" title="Philip Yorke, 2nd Earl of Hardwicke">Lord Hardwicke</a>, leader of the "Hardwicke Circle" that dominated society politics during the 1750s and '60s</div></div></div>
<p>During the 18th century, the gusto that had characterised the early years of the society faded; with a small number of scientific "greats" compared to other periods, little of note was done. In the second half, it became customary for <a href="/wiki/His_Majesty%27s_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="His Majesty&#39;s Government">His Majesty's Government</a> to refer highly important scientific questions to the council of the society for advice, something that, despite the non-partisan nature of the society, spilled into politics in 1777 over <a href="/wiki/Lightning_conductor" class="mw-redirect" title="Lightning conductor">lightning conductors</a>. The pointed lightning conductor had been invented by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> in 1749, while <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Wilson_(painter)" title="Benjamin Wilson (painter)">Benjamin Wilson</a> invented blunted ones. During the argument that occurred when deciding which to use, opponents of Franklin's invention accused supporters of being American allies rather than being British, and the debate eventually led to the resignation of the society's president, <a href="/wiki/John_Pringle_(physician)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Pringle (physician)">Sir John Pringle</a>. During the same time period, it became customary to appoint society fellows to serve on government committees where science was concerned, something that still continues.<sup id="cite_ref-hend2_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hend2-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 18th century featured remedies to many of the society's early problems. The number of fellows had increased from 110 to approximately 300 by 1739, the reputation of the society had increased under the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a> from 1703 until his death in 1727,<sup id="cite_ref-newt_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newt-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and editions of the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society">Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society</a></i> were appearing regularly.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> During his time as president, Newton arguably abused his authority; in a dispute between himself and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" class="mw-redirect" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a> over the invention of <a href="/wiki/Infinitesimal_calculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Infinitesimal calculus">infinitesimal calculus</a>, he used his position to appoint an "impartial" committee to decide it, eventually publishing a report written by himself in the committee's name.<sup id="cite_ref-newt_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newt-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In 1705, the society was informed that it could no longer rent Gresham College and began a search for new premises. After unsuccessfully applying to <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a> for new premises, and asking the trustees of Cotton House if they could meet there, the council bought two houses in Crane Court, <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Street" title="Fleet Street">Fleet Street</a>, on 26 October 1710.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This included offices, accommodation and a collection of curiosities. Although the overall fellowship contained few noted scientists, most of the council were highly regarded, and included at various times <a href="/wiki/John_Hadley" title="John Hadley">John Hadley</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Jones_(mathematician)" title="William Jones (mathematician)">William Jones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Sloane" title="Hans Sloane">Hans Sloane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Because of the laxness of fellows in paying their subscriptions, the society ran into financial difficulty during this time; by 1740, the society had a deficit of £240. This continued into 1741, at which point the treasurer began dealing harshly with fellows who had not paid.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The business of the society at this time continued to include the demonstration of experiments and the reading of formal and important scientific papers, along with the demonstration of new scientific devices and queries about scientific matters from both Britain and Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some modern research has asserted that the claims of the society's degradation during the 18th century are false. Richard Sorrenson writes that "far from having 'fared ingloriously', the society experienced a period of significant productivity and growth throughout the eighteenth century", pointing out that many of the sources critical accounts are based on are in fact written by those with an agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Charles_Babbage" title="Charles Babbage">Charles Babbage</a> wrote that the practice of pure mathematics in Britain was weak, laying the blame at the doorstep of the society, the practice of mixed mathematics was strong and although there were not many eminent members of the society, some did contribute vast amounts&#160;– <a href="/wiki/James_Bradley" title="James Bradley">James Bradley</a>, for example, established the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_nutation" title="Astronomical nutation">nutation of the Earth's axis</a> with 20 years of detailed, meticulous astronomy.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Politically within the society, the mid-18th century featured a "<a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whig</a> supremacy" as the so-called "Hardwicke Circle" of Whig-leaning scientists held the society's main Offices. Named after <a href="/wiki/Philip_Yorke,_2nd_Earl_of_Hardwicke" title="Philip Yorke, 2nd Earl of Hardwicke">Lord Hardwicke</a>, the group's members included <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Wray" title="Daniel Wray">Daniel Wray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Birch" title="Thomas Birch">Thomas Birch</a> and was most prominent in the 1750s and '60s. The circle had Birch elected secretary and, following the resignation of <a href="/wiki/Martin_Folkes" title="Martin Folkes">Martin Folkes</a>, the circle helped oversee a smooth transition to the presidency of <a href="/wiki/George_Parker,_2nd_Earl_of_Macclesfield" title="George Parker, 2nd Earl of Macclesfield">Earl Macclesfield</a>, whom Hardwicke helped elect.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Under Macclesfield, the circle reached its "zenith", with members such as Lord Willoughby and Birch serving as vice-president and secretary respectively. The circle also influenced goings-on in other learned societies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Antiquaries_of_London" title="Society of Antiquaries of London">Society of Antiquaries of London</a>. After Macclesfield's retirement, the circle had <a href="/wiki/James_Douglas,_14th_Earl_of_Morton" title="James Douglas, 14th Earl of Morton">Lord Morton</a> elected in 1764 and <a href="/wiki/John_Pringle_(physician)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Pringle (physician)">Sir John Pringle</a> elected in 1772.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> By this point, the previous Whig "majority" had been reduced to a "faction", with Birch and Willoughby no longer involved, and the circle declined in the same time frame as the political party did in British politics under <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a>, falling apart in the 1780s.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1780, the society moved again, this time to <a href="/wiki/Somerset_House" title="Somerset House">Somerset House</a>. The property was offered to the society by His Majesty's Government and, as soon as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Banks" title="Joseph Banks">Sir Joseph Banks</a> became president in November 1778, he began planning the move. Somerset House, while larger than Crane Court, was not satisfying to the fellows; the room to store the library was too small, the accommodation was insufficient and there was not enough room to store the museum at all. As a result, the museum was handed to the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> in 1781 and the library was extended to two rooms, one of which was used for council meetings.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century_to_the_present">19th century to the present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 19th century to the present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burlington_House_ILN_1873.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Burlington_House_ILN_1873.jpg/220px-Burlington_House_ILN_1873.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Burlington_House_ILN_1873.jpg/330px-Burlington_House_ILN_1873.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Burlington_House_ILN_1873.jpg/440px-Burlington_House_ILN_1873.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="413" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burlington_House_ILN_1873.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Burlington_House" title="Burlington House">Burlington House</a>, where the Society was based between 1873 and 1967</div></div></div>
<p>The early 19th century has been seen as a time of decline for the society; of 662 fellows in 1830, only 104 had contributed to the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society">Philosophical Transactions</a></i>. The same year, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Babbage" title="Charles Babbage">Charles Babbage</a> published <i>Reflections on the Decline of Science in England, and on Some of Its Causes</i>, which was deeply critical of the Society. The scientific Fellows of the Society were spurred into action by this, and eventually <a href="/wiki/James_South" title="James South">James South</a> established a Charters Committee "with a view to obtaining a supplementary Charter from the Crown", aimed primarily at looking at ways to restrict membership. The Committee recommended that the election of Fellows take place on one day every year, that the Fellows be selected on consideration of their scientific achievements and that the number of fellows elected a year be limited to 15. This limit was increased to 17 in 1930 and 20 in 1937;<sup id="cite_ref-hend2_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hend2-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> it is currently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2016)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> 52.<sup id="cite_ref-fellow_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fellow-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This had a number of effects on the Society: first, the Society's membership became almost entirely scientific, with few political Fellows or patrons. Second, the number of Fellows was significantly reduced—between 1700 and 1850, the number of Fellows rose from approximately 100 to approximately 750. From then until 1941, the total number of Fellows was always between 400 and 500.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The period did lead to some reform of internal Society statutes, such as in 1823 and 1831. The most important change there was the requirement that the Treasurer publish an annual report, along with a copy of the total income and expenditure of the Society. These were to be sent to Fellows at least 14 days before the general meeting, with the intent being to ensure the election of competent Officers by making it readily apparent what existing Officers were doing. This was accompanied by a full list of Fellows standing for Council positions, where previously the names had only been announced a couple of days before. As with the other reforms, this helped ensure that Fellows had a chance to vet and properly consider candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The Society's financial troubles were finally resolved in 1850, when a government <a href="/wiki/Grant-in-aid" title="Grant-in-aid">grant-in-aid</a> of £1,000 a year was accepted. This was increased to £4,000 in 1876, with the Society officially acting merely as the trustee for these funds, doling them out to individual scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This grant has now grown to over £47&#160;million, some £37&#160;million of which is to support around 370 fellowships and professorships.<sup id="cite_ref-Parliamentary_Grant_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parliamentary_Grant-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1852, the congestion at <a href="/wiki/Somerset_House" title="Somerset House">Somerset House</a> had increased thanks to the growing number of Fellows. Therefore, the Library Committee asked the Council to petition Her Majesty's Government to find new facilities, with the advice being to bring all the scientific societies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Linnean_Society_of_London" title="Linnean Society of London">Linnean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geological_Society_of_London" title="Geological Society of London">Geological</a> societies, under one roof. In August 1866, the government announced their intention to refurbish <a href="/wiki/Burlington_House" title="Burlington House">Burlington House</a> and move the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> and other societies there. The Academy moved in 1867, while other societies joined when their facilities were built. The Royal Society moved there in 1873, taking up residence in the East Wing.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The top floor was used as accommodation for the Assistant Secretary, while the library was scattered over every room and the old caretaker's apartment was converted into offices. One flaw was that there was not enough space for the office staff, which was then approximately eighty. When, for example, the Society organised the British contribution to the <a href="/wiki/International_Geophysical_Year" title="International Geophysical Year">International Geophysical Year</a> in 1954, additional facilities had to be found for the staff outside Burlington House.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 22 March 1945, the first female Fellows were elected to the Royal Society. This followed a statutory amendment in 1944 that read "Nothing herein contained shall render women ineligible as candidates", and was contained in Chapter 1 of Statute 1. Because of the difficulty of co-ordinating all the Fellows during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, a ballot on making the change was conducted via the post, with 336 Fellows supporting the change and 37 opposing.<sup id="cite_ref-RS1946_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS1946-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Following approval by the Council, <a href="/wiki/Marjory_Stephenson" title="Marjory Stephenson">Marjory Stephenson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Lonsdale" title="Kathleen Lonsdale">Kathleen Lonsdale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edith_B%C3%BClbring" title="Edith Bülbring">Edith Bülbring</a> were elected as Fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-RS1946_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS1946-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coat_of_arms">Coat of arms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Coat of arms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arms_of_the_Royal_Society.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Arms_of_the_Royal_Society.svg/170px-Arms_of_the_Royal_Society.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Arms_of_the_Royal_Society.svg/255px-Arms_of_the_Royal_Society.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Arms_of_the_Royal_Society.svg/340px-Arms_of_the_Royal_Society.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="483" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arms_of_the_Royal_Society.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a> of the Royal Society</div></div></div>
<p>The blazon for the shield in the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a> of the Royal Society is <i>in a dexter corner of a shield argent our three Lions of England, and for crest a helm adorned with a crown studded with florets, surmounted by an eagle of proper colour holding in one foot a shield charged with our lions: supporters two white hounds gorged with crowns</i>, with the motto of <i><a href="/wiki/Nullius_in_verba" title="Nullius in verba">nullius in verba</a></i>. John Evelyn, interested in the early structure of the society, had sketched out at least six possible designs, but in August 1662 <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> told the society that it was allowed to use the arms of England as part of its coat and the society "now resolv'd that the armes of the Society should be, <i>a field Argent, with a canton of the armes of England; the supporters two talbots Argent</i>; Crest, <i>an eagle Or holding a shield with the like armes of England, viz. 3 lions</i>. The words <i>Nullius in verba</i>". This was approved by Charles, who asked <a href="/wiki/Garter_King_of_Arms" class="mw-redirect" title="Garter King of Arms">Garter King of Arms</a> to create a diploma for it, and when the second charter was signed on 22 April 1663 the arms were granted to the president, council and fellows of the society along with their successors.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The helmet of the arms was not specified in the charter, but the engraver sketched out a <a href="/wiki/Helmet_(heraldry)" title="Helmet (heraldry)">peer's helmet</a> on the final design, which is used. This is contrary to the heraldic rules, as a society or corporation normally has an esquire's helmet; it is thought that either the engraver was ignorant of this rule, which was not strictly adhered to until around 1615, or that he used the peer's helmet as a compliment to <a href="/wiki/William_Brouncker,_2nd_Viscount_Brouncker" title="William Brouncker, 2nd Viscount Brouncker">Lord Brouncker</a>, a peer and the first President of the Royal Society.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Motto">Motto</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Motto">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The society's motto, <i><a href="/wiki/Nullius_in_verba" title="Nullius in verba">Nullius in verba</a></i>, is <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "Take nobody's word for it". It was adopted to signify the fellows' determination to establish facts via experiments and comes from <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Epistles_(Horace)" title="Epistles (Horace)">Epistles</a></i>, where he compares himself to a gladiator who, having retired, is free from control.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_.28FRS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_(FRS)">Fellows of the Royal Society (FRS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Fellows of the Royal Society (FRS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">Fellow of the Royal Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society" title="List of Fellows of the Royal Society">List of Fellows of the Royal Society</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_female_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society" title="List of female Fellows of the Royal Society">List of female Fellows of the Royal Society</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J.J_Thomson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/J.J_Thomson.jpg/170px-J.J_Thomson.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/J.J_Thomson.jpg/255px-J.J_Thomson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/J.J_Thomson.jpg/340px-J.J_Thomson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1563" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:J.J_Thomson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a> was elected to Fellow of the Royal Society in 1884.</div></div></div>
<p>The society's core members are the fellows: scientists and engineers from the United Kingdom and the Commonwealth selected based on having made "a substantial contribution to the improvement of natural knowledge, including mathematics, engineering science and medical science".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Fellows are elected for life and gain the right to use the <a href="/wiki/Post-nominal_letters" title="Post-nominal letters">postnominal</a> <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">Fellow of the Royal Society</a> (FRS). The rights and responsibilities of fellows also include a duty to financially contribute to the society, the right to stand for council posts and the right to elect new fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Up to 52 fellows are elected each year and in 2014 there were about 1,450 living members in total.<sup id="cite_ref-fellow_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fellow-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Election to the fellowship is decided by ten sectional committees (each covering a subject area or set of subjects areas) which consist of existing fellows.
</p><p>The society also elects royal fellows, honorary fellows and foreign members. Royal fellows are those members of the <a href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="British Royal Family">British Royal Family</a>, representing the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">British monarchy</a>'s role in promoting and supporting the society, who are recommended by the society's council and elected via postal vote. There are currently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2016)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> five royal fellows: <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">The Duke of Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">The Prince of Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward,_Duke_of_Kent" title="Prince Edward, Duke of Kent">The Duke of Kent</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Princess_Royal" title="Anne, Princess Royal">Princess Royal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prince_William,_Duke_of_Cambridge" title="Prince William, Duke of Cambridge">The Duke of Cambridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Honorary fellows are people who are ineligible to be elected as fellows but nevertheless have "rendered signal service to the cause of science, or whose election would significantly benefit the Society by their great experience in other walks of life". Six honorary fellows have been elected to date, including <a href="/wiki/Onora_O%27Neill,_Baroness_O%27Neill_of_Bengarve" class="mw-redirect" title="Onora O&#39;Neill, Baroness O&#39;Neill of Bengarve">Baroness O'Neill of Bengarve</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Foreign members are scientists from non-Commonwealth nations "who are eminent for their scientific discoveries and attainments". Eight are elected each year by the society and also hold their membership for life. Foreign members are permitted to use the post-nominal ForMemRS (Foreign Member of the Royal Society) and currently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2016)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> number about 140.<sup id="cite_ref-ForMemRS_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ForMemRS-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stephen_Hawking.StarChild.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Stephen_Hawking.StarChild.jpg/170px-Stephen_Hawking.StarChild.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Stephen_Hawking.StarChild.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="359" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stephen_Hawking.StarChild.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> was elected a Fellow of the Royal Society in 1974</div></div></div>
<p>The appointment of fellows was first authorised in the second charter, issued on 22 April 1663, which allowed the president and council, in the two months following the signing, to appoint as fellows any individuals they saw fit. This saw the appointment of 94 fellows on 20 May and 4 on 22 June; these 98 are known as the "Original Fellows". After the expiration of this two-month period any appointments were to be made by the president, council and existing fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Many early fellows were not scientists or particularly eminent intellectuals; it was clear that the early society could not rely on financial assistance from the king, and scientifically trained fellows were few and far between. It was therefore necessary to secure the favour of wealthy or important individuals for the society's survival.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> While the entrance fee of £4 and the subscription rate of one <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shilling</a> a week should have produced £600 a year for the society, many fellows paid neither regularly nor on time.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Two-thirds of the fellows in 1663 were non-scientists; this rose to 71.6% in 1800 before dropping to 47.4% in 1860 as the financial security of the society became more certain.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In May 1846, a committee recommended limiting the annual intake of members to 15 and insisting on scientific eminence; this was implemented, with the result being that the society now consists exclusively of scientific fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_governance">Structure and governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Structure and governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The society is governed by its council, which is chaired by the society's <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_Royal_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the Royal Society">president</a>, according to a set of statutes and standing orders. The members of council, the president and the other officers are elected from and by its fellowship.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Council">Council</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Council">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The council is a body of 21 fellows, including the officers (the president, the treasurer, two secretaries—one from the physical sciences, one from life sciences—and the foreign secretary),<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> one fellow to represent each sectional committee and seven other fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-con_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-con-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The council is tasked with directing the society's overall policy, managing all business related to the society, amending, making or repealing the society's standing orders and acting as trustees for the society's possessions and estates. Members are elected annually via a postal ballot, and current standing orders mean that at least ten seats must change hands each year.<sup id="cite_ref-so_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-so-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The council may establish (and is assisted by) a variety of committees,<sup id="cite_ref-so_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-so-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> which can include not only fellows but also outside scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-con_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-con-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Under the charter, the president, two secretaries and the treasurer are collectively the officers of the society.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The current officers<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> are:
</p>
<ul><li>President: <a href="/wiki/Venkatraman_Ramakrishnan" title="Venkatraman Ramakrishnan">Venkatraman Ramakrishnan</a></li>
<li>Treasurer: <a href="/wiki/Andy_Hopper" title="Andy Hopper">Andy Hopper</a></li>
<li>Biological Secretary: <a href="/wiki/John_Skehel" title="John Skehel">Sir John Skehel</a></li>
<li>Physical Secretary&#160;: <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bruce" title="Peter Bruce">Peter Bruce</a></li>
<li>Foreign Secretary: <a href="/wiki/Richard_Catlow" title="Richard Catlow">Richard Catlow</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="President">President</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: President">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_presidents_of_the_Royal_Society" title="List of presidents of the Royal Society">List of presidents of the Royal Society</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venki_Ramakrishnan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Venki_Ramakrishnan.jpg/170px-Venki_Ramakrishnan.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Venki_Ramakrishnan.jpg/255px-Venki_Ramakrishnan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Venki_Ramakrishnan.jpg/340px-Venki_Ramakrishnan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="1772" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venki_Ramakrishnan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Venkatraman_Ramakrishnan" title="Venkatraman Ramakrishnan">Venkatraman Ramakrishnan</a> has been President of the Society since 2015</div></div></div>
<p>The President of the Royal Society is head of both the society and the council. The details for the presidency were set out in the second charter and initially had no limit on how long a president could serve for; under current society statute, the term is five years.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The current president is <a href="/wiki/Venkatraman_Ramakrishnan" title="Venkatraman Ramakrishnan">Venkatraman Ramakrishnan</a>, who took over from <a href="/wiki/Paul_Nurse" title="Paul Nurse">Paul Nurse</a> on 30 November 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Historically, the duties of the president have been both formal and social. The <a href="/wiki/Cruelty_to_Animals_Act_1876" class="mw-redirect" title="Cruelty to Animals Act 1876">Cruelty to Animals Act 1876</a> left the president as one of the few individuals capable of certifying that a particular experiment on an animal was justified. In addition, the president is to act as the government's chief (albeit informal) advisor on scientific matters. Yet another task is that of entertaining distinguished foreign guests and scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Permanent_staff">Permanent staff</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Permanent staff">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The society is assisted by a number of full-time paid staff. The original charter provided for "two or more Operators of Experiments, and two or more clerks"; as the number of books in the society's collection grew, it also became necessary to employ a curator. The staff grew as the financial position of the society improved, mainly consisting of outsiders, along with a small number of scientists who were required to resign their fellowship on employment.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The current Executive Director is <a href="/wiki/Julie_Maxton" title="Julie Maxton">Julie Maxton</a> and the Director of Science Policy is <a href="/wiki/Claire_Craig" title="Claire Craig">Claire Craig</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ss_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ss-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions_and_activities">Functions and activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Functions and activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Society_Collections_at_History_Day_2016.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Royal_Society_Collections_at_History_Day_2016.jpg/220px-Royal_Society_Collections_at_History_Day_2016.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Royal_Society_Collections_at_History_Day_2016.jpg/330px-Royal_Society_Collections_at_History_Day_2016.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Royal_Society_Collections_at_History_Day_2016.jpg/440px-Royal_Society_Collections_at_History_Day_2016.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Society_Collections_at_History_Day_2016.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Royal Society Collections at the University of London History Day, 2016.</div></div></div>
<p>The society has a variety of functions and activities. It supports modern science by disbursing nearly £42&#160;million to fund approximately 600 research fellowships for both early and late career scientists, along with innovation, mobility and research capacity grants.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Awards,_lectures_and_medals_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Awards, lectures and medals of the Royal Society">Awards, prize lectures and medals</a> all come with prize money intended to finance research,<sup id="cite_ref-prs_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prs-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and it provides subsidised communications and media skills courses for research scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Much of this activity is supported by a grant from the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Business,_Innovation_and_Skills" title="Department for Business, Innovation and Skills">Department for Business, Innovation and Skills</a>, most of which is channelled to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_University_Research_Fellowship" title="Royal Society University Research Fellowship">University Research Fellowships (URF)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Parliamentary_Grant_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parliamentary_Grant-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, the society opened the Royal Society Enterprise Fund, intended to invest in new scientific companies and be self-sustaining, funded (after an initial set of donations on the 350th anniversary of the society) by the returns from its investments.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Through its Science Policy Centre, the society acts as an advisor to the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">UK Government</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> on matters of science. It publishes several reports a year, and serves as the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy of Sciences">Academy of Sciences</a> of the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Since the middle of the 18th century, government problems involving science were irregularly referred to the Society, and by 1800 it was done regularly.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Carlton_House_Terrace">Carlton House Terrace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Carlton House Terrace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Society_20040420.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Royal_Society_20040420.jpg/220px-Royal_Society_20040420.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Royal_Society_20040420.jpg/330px-Royal_Society_20040420.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Royal_Society_20040420.jpg/440px-Royal_Society_20040420.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1437" data-file-height="998" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Society_20040420.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The current premises of the Royal Society, 6–9 <a href="/wiki/Carlton_House_Terrace" title="Carlton House Terrace">Carlton House Terrace</a>, London (first four properties only)</div></div></div>
<p>The premises at 6–9 <a href="/wiki/Carlton_House_Terrace" title="Carlton House Terrace">Carlton House Terrace</a> is a Grade I listed building and the current headquarters of the Royal Society, which had moved there from <a href="/wiki/Burlington_House" title="Burlington House">Burlington House</a> in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-general_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-general-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The ground floor and basement are used for ceremonies, social and publicity events, the first floor hosts facilities for Fellows and Officers of the Society, and the second and third floors are divided between offices and accommodation for the President, Executive Secretary and Fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-fish1_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fish1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Carlton House was named after <a href="/wiki/Henry_Boyle,_1st_Baron_Carleton" title="Henry Boyle, 1st Baron Carleton">Baron Carleton</a>, and was sold to <a href="/wiki/Philip_Stanhope,_4th_Earl_of_Chesterfield" title="Philip Stanhope, 4th Earl of Chesterfield">Lord Chesterfield</a> in 1732, who held it <a href="/wiki/English_trusts_law" class="mw-redirect" title="English trusts law">on trust</a> for <a href="/wiki/Frederick,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Frederick, Prince of Wales">Frederick, Prince of Wales</a>. Frederick held his court there until his death in 1751, after which it was occupied by his widow until her death in 1772. In 1783, the then-Prince of Wales <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George</a> bought the house, instructing his architect <a href="/wiki/Henry_Holland_(architect)" title="Henry Holland (architect)">Henry Holland</a> to completely remodel it.
</p><p>When George became King, he authorised the demolition of Carlton House, with the request that the replacement be a residential area. <a href="/wiki/John_Nash_(architect)" title="John Nash (architect)">John Nash</a> eventually completed a design that saw Carlton House turned into two blocks of houses, with a space in between them.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The building is still owned by the <a href="/wiki/Crown_Estates" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Estates">Crown Estates</a> and leased by the Society; it underwent a major renovation from 2001 to 2004 at the cost of £9.8&#160;million, and was reopened by <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">the Prince of Wales</a> on 7 July 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-pw_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pw-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Carlton House Terrace underwent a series of renovations between 1999 and November 2003 to improve and standardise the property. New waiting, exhibition and reception rooms were created in the house at No.7, using the Magna Boschi marble found in No.8, and greenish grey Statuario Venato marble was used in other areas to standardise the design.<sup id="cite_ref-fish1_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fish1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> An effort was also made to make the layout of the buildings easier, consolidating all the offices on one floor, Fellows' Rooms on another and all the accommodation on a third.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kavli_Royal_Society_International_Centre">Kavli Royal Society International Centre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Kavli Royal Society International Centre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2009 <a href="/wiki/Chicheley_Hall" title="Chicheley Hall">Chicheley Hall</a>, a Grade I listed building located near <a href="/wiki/Milton_Keynes" title="Milton Keynes">Milton Keynes</a>, was bought by the Royal Society for £6.5&#160;million, funded in part by the <a href="/wiki/Kavli_Foundation_(United_States)" title="Kavli Foundation (United States)">Kavli Foundation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The Royal Society spent several million on renovations adapting it to become the Kavli Royal Society International Centre, a venue for residential science seminars. The centre held its first scientific meeting on 1 June 2010 and was formally opened on 21 June 2010.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Publishing">Publishing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Publishing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Philosophical_Transactions_Volume_1_frontispiece.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philosophical_Transactions_Volume_1_frontispiece.jpg/220px-Philosophical_Transactions_Volume_1_frontispiece.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philosophical_Transactions_Volume_1_frontispiece.jpg/330px-Philosophical_Transactions_Volume_1_frontispiece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philosophical_Transactions_Volume_1_frontispiece.jpg/440px-Philosophical_Transactions_Volume_1_frontispiece.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="2054" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Philosophical_Transactions_Volume_1_frontispiece.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of the first edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society">Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society</a></i> published in 1665</div></div></div>
<p>The society introduced the world's first journal exclusively devoted to science in 1665, <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society">Philosophical Transactions</a></i>, and in so doing originated the <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer review</a> process now widespread in scientific journals. Its founding editor was <a href="/wiki/Henry_Oldenburg" title="Henry Oldenburg">Henry Oldenburg</a>, the society's first secretary.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Through Royal Society Publishing, the society publishes the following journals:<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society"><i>Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society A</i></a> (mathematics and the physical sciences)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society"><i>Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society B</i></a> (biological sciences)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Proceedings of the Royal Society"><i>Proceedings of the Royal Society A</i></a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Proceedings of the Royal Society"><i>Proceedings of the Royal Society B</i></a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Biology_Letters" title="Biology Letters">Biology Letters</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Open_Biology" title="Open Biology">Open Biology</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_Open_Science" title="Royal Society Open Science">Royal Society Open Science</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_Royal_Society_Interface" title="Journal of the Royal Society Interface">Journal of the Royal Society Interface</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Interface_Focus" title="Interface Focus">Interface Focus</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Notes_and_Records" title="Notes and Records">Notes and Records</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Biographical_Memoirs_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Biographical Memoirs of Fellows of the Royal Society">Biographical Memoirs of Fellows of the Royal Society</a></i></li></ul>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society">Philosophical Transactions</a></i> is the oldest and longest-running scientific journal in the world, having first been published in March 1665 by the first secretary of the society, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Oldenburg" title="Henry Oldenburg">Henry Oldenburg</a>. It now publishes themed issues on specific topics and, since 1886,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> has been divided into two parts; A, which deals with mathematics and the physical sciences,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> and B, which deals with the biological sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Proceedings of the Royal Society">Proceedings of the Royal Society</a></i> consists of freely submitted research articles and is similarly divided into two parts.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Biology_Letters" title="Biology Letters">Biology Letters</a></i> publishes short research articles and opinion pieces on all areas of biology and was launched in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_Royal_Society_Interface" title="Journal of the Royal Society Interface">Journal of the Royal Society Interface</a></i> publishes cross-disciplinary research at the boundary between the physical and life sciences,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/Interface_Focus" title="Interface Focus">Interface Focus</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> publishes themed issue in the same areas. <i><a href="/wiki/Notes_and_Records" title="Notes and Records">Notes and Records</a></i> is the Society's journal of the history of science.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biographical_Memoirs_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Biographical Memoirs of Fellows of the Royal Society"><i>Biographical Memoirs</i></a> is published twice annually and contains extended obituaries of deceased Fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Open_Biology" title="Open Biology">Open Biology</a></i> is an <a href="/wiki/Open_access_journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Open access journal">open access journal</a> covering <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cellular level</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_Open_Science" title="Royal Society Open Science">Royal Society Open Science</a></i> is an <a href="/wiki/Open_access_journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Open access journal">open access journal</a> publishing high-quality original research across the entire range of science on the basis of objective peer-review.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> All the society's journals are <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer-reviewed</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours">Honours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Honours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Awards,_lectures_and_medals_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Awards, lectures and medals of the Royal Society">Awards, lectures and medals of the Royal Society</a></div>
<p>The Royal Society presents numerous awards, lectures and medals to recognise scientific achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-prs_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prs-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The oldest is the <a href="/wiki/Croonian_Lecture" title="Croonian Lecture">Croonian Lecture</a>, created in 1701 at the request of the widow of <a href="/wiki/William_Croone" title="William Croone">William Croone</a>, one of the founding members of the Royal Society. The Croonian Lecture is still awarded on an annual basis, and is considered the most important Royal Society prize for the biological sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Although the Croonian Lecture was created in 1701, it was first awarded in 1738, seven years after the <a href="/wiki/Copley_Medal" title="Copley Medal">Copley Medal</a>. The Copley Medal is the oldest Royal Society medal still in use and is awarded for "outstanding achievements in research in any branch of science".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
